### Post by joey-elijah on 2009-11-18
So the plan, whilst obviously not 100% final, is to ditch the GIMP from Lucid. 
[URL="http://www.omgubuntu.co.uk/2009/11/gimp-to-be-removed-lucid.html"]
http://www.omgubuntu.co.uk/2009/11/gimp-to-be-removed-lucid.html[/URL]

I think this is a good call - it uses space, the average user never uses it to its potential and could easily do what they need to in other applications. 

I'd still rather we got rid of F-Spot, too though. It's a lame exucse of a photo manager...

Not sold on PiTiVi as the best choice of video editor. The tone of the meeting about default apps was very heavily decided in favour of trying pitivi, and OpenShot wasn't seriously considered despite the fact it's, imo at least, the better of the two.

---

### Post by amauk on 2009-11-18

@CoreyB

It's less about CD vs. DVD, and more about not overloading a default install

### Post by Arup on 2009-11-18

---

### Post by lonniehenry on 2009-11-18
I use Gimp, however the average user doesn't.  Given that it will continue to be available to everyone, dropping it from the default will free up space on the cd.  Keeping the distro to a cd in size will help force the devs to keep streamlining the os.  If the default distro was allowed to grow to a dvd size, the chance for bloating would increase.  

The framework limiting the distro to a cd has worked in the past to keep Ubuntu to a minimum hardware requirements that most computers can handle - even low powered older machines.

---

### Post by gnomeuser on 2009-11-18
I always wondered why including a complicated photoshop type application by default is considered such a good idea. 

Your average user might want to touch up apps, turn them into calendars or christmas cards. These tasks could easily fit in your photo management application. GIMP really is too powerful for this task, it's a professional quality graphics application - people who need it can install it and know how to get it. We could even extend the software center to present valuable extra apps or something like a "creativity add on" which could install the best of breed such apps like blender, gimp and so on along perhaps with some instructional videos and sample content created with the tools.

---

### Post by Reiger on 2009-11-19
I like that idea of ditching GIMP as default. GIMP is undoubtedly a very capable program; but it is not something most people would actually need.

Including the GIMP by default is much like including an entire LAMP stack & webdevelopment toolchain by default. Quite cool for those who are into webdevelopment; but quite useless for those who are not.

On the subject of a nice image editing program. I like the combo of Kolourpaint/Gwenview... But that is KDE...

### Post by saulgoode on 2009-11-19
> **Acer3810T said:**
> That can't be right ... can it? 1/10 of the CD used just for gimp? Unless you're talking about installed size?

In 9.10, the gimp package itself is 6.7Mb (including gimp-data-extras). The GIMP help files account for an addition 15.5Mb (English only, other languages would require about 9Mb per locale but are not included on the ISO).

GIMP is able to access help files from the web if the computer has a connection. GIMP is otherwise fully functional without help installed or accessible.

---

### Post by dvlchd3 on 2009-11-19
I believe this is a good idea.  Even if you are a Windows user and use Photoshop, seeing GIMP is not going to make you want to install Ubuntu, it may actually deter you.  Moving from PS to Gimp is not easy, many tools are labeled different and in different places than PS.  And trust me, graphic designers are picky!  :-P

As pointed out previously, most users don't need most of the features of Gimp, and will probably have to go buy a book to fully understand them, or PS for that matter.  I believe some application that allows easy manipulation of photos should be the default, i.e. - cropping images, reducing redeye, etc.  Although I'm not familiar with any of applications that would meet these needs, since I do primarily use the GIMP, I'm sure they exist, and if they don't, we should start one!

### Post by Nerd King on 2009-11-19
I think you guys are forgetting something. The LiveCD isn't just an installation disk. It's also a showcase for what Ubuntu and linux in general can do. New users who try the live cd will base their opinion on what they see in the first 10 minutes or so. Gimp and openoffice are vital to dragging in the newbies and if Ubuntu's goal is world domination then that's what we need. Remember one of the selling points is having all the tools most of us need straight away without having to install anything. It's as important as the cube, a decent wallpaper and all those other things. Sure us experienced users aren't affected if the cd loses these things, but the liveCD is there to catch the eye, so let it do so.

> **emigrant said:**

Oh? Wanna bet? 

As long as there is an internet connection set up prior to installing you install anything. And that does not require any software on any dvd.

### Post by ronacc on 2009-11-19
The goal they aim at with the cd is to have a "standalone" install from the cd with no internet connection required . This is because the "target market" for Ubuntu includes people/places that do not have easy access to a cheap or fast connection or any access at all .Remember you can get the cd by mail so that it does't have to be downloaded .The same rationale is why we stick to a cd instead of a dvd , some people still don't have dvd drives .

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2009-11-19
The problem is not that GIMP is "too complicated." It's that GIMP is targeted at a relatively small group of users. Additionally the Popcon data shows us that GIMP is not enormously popular. (More analysis would be nice, indeed).
Removing GIMP allows some room for new stuff: a nice video editor, for example. (Probably going to be Pitivi). The selection of games was also shifted around in the discussion.

In the discussion behind this idea, some great stuff was mentioned. Right now we use the default menu as a dumping ground for stuff that is simply awesome and we want to loudly demonstrate that Ubuntu has. For example, GIMP.
However, that isn't how it should be! We now have an awesome, easy and beautiful Software Centre feature that can serve that purpose and also really "sell" the apps by describing their key features (instead of just "image editor").

Personally, I'm all for the move!

Now, the part I'm not big on is adopting F-Spot Editing Mode as the only image editor in by default. I pointed out during the session that it is not currently suited for the task since it offers very little control in a very fiddly and inconsistent way and there is no obvious undo feature. There is a revert feature (by deleting the current version, which is scary), but that is not sufficient.
So, it needs some love :)

---

### Post by alphaniner on 2009-11-19

### Post by J-Buntu on 2009-11-19
IMO it would be a good idea to maybe have a "suggested installation selection package" for people new to Ubuntu. This could maybe be a separate option in the software centre, possibly an option that could be unchecked later, once people felt comfortable to explore on their own. This selection could include obvious favourites, such as ccsm, fusion-icon, gimp, open office, abiword, evo mail, vlc, banshee, chromium, various messengers, stellarium, a few more games - whatever. Just some worthwhile alternatives to get people going, because some people just see the "default Ubuntu" - and assume that's all there is. First impressions count a lot of course. 

I'm just thinking about the vast amounts of new-comers that seem to be allergic to google. I think that's a similar idea to what someone else was suggesting.

### Post by tekstr1der on 2009-11-19
> **ronacc said:**
> The goal they aim at with the cd is to have a "standalone" install from the cd with no internet connection required . This is because the "target market" for Ubuntu includes people/places that do not have easy access to a cheap or fast connection or any access at all .Remember you can get the cd by mail so that it does't have to be downloaded .The same rationale is why we stick to a cd instead of a dvd , some people still don't have dvd drives .

If that's the case, why must ubiquity download language packs during installation? This can add considerable time to an install. I suppose I could understand this if you're intending to use an uncommon language, but this happens with the default English US install. While skipping this step is an option, it not only requires user interaction far into the process, but causes an error to be displayed immediately upon first logon.

Having an installer that does not require an Internet connection and mandatory downloads should be a serious priority. If removing GIMP from the ISO can help accomplish this goal, I'm all for it.

### Post by Dougie187 on 2009-11-19
> **J-Buntu said:**
> IMO it would be a good idea to maybe have a "suggested installation selection package" for people new to Ubuntu. This could maybe be a separate option in the software centre, possibly an option that could be unchecked later, once people felt comfortable to explore on their own. This selection could include obvious favourites, such as ccsm, fusion-icon, gimp, open office, abiword, evo mail, vlc, banshee, chromium, various messengers, stellarium, a few more games - whatever. Just some worthwhile alternatives to get people going, because some people just see the "default Ubuntu" - and assume that's all there is. First impressions count a lot of course. 

I'm just thinking about the vast amounts of new-comers that seem to be allergic to google. I think that's a similar idea to what someone else was suggesting.

I don't think this is a good idea. The reason is because right now ubuntu has a default set of applications and layout that they use, and it makes sense, it's easy to navigate and figure out what you want to do. If you start throwing in redundant applications just so new users know that they exist first off you will have way too many useless programs for most people, and second people who don't know what any of them are will just be confused as to which one to use and why. Like take for example just installing Firefox and Chromium, and possibly even Opera in a "suggested" package configuration. You install this on your grandmothers computer, and she sees three web browsers on it, but can't figure out which one to use. It makes the OS seem a lot more cluttered kind of like the people who were developing it couldn't make up their mind to pick one and just threw them all it.

Kind of like a bad video game montage.

Now, something that might be interesting, would be to have 'common alternatives' or something listed in the Software Center. Maybe as an option from install software. Then hopefully at some point it will include reviews and ratings as well so lets say your grandma again, could go into the software center and look for alternative browsers easily, then get ratings on each to choose which one she would want to use.

Obviously i wasn't suggesting all these programs should be installed as a bundle of software - or what would be the point of having it separate ? The idea is that they are placed together as a selection - to be chosen by the user, individually, or as required. I know a rating system exists currently, but it's unclear to the layman what to choose and maybe viewed as overwhelming. 

It's just to cut down on the size of the installation - hence the point of the thread. A place to view a more limited selection of users favourites, that have been left out, but come highly recommended.

It would make more room for OS stuff instead of software. also dumb users have no idea how to use repository, and even if they did they wouldn't know what software to use. That's why good defaults are important. No image editor is a bad default.

### Post by ticopelp on 2009-11-19
I wouldn't say it's a "major step back," but I don't think it's a good idea. If you look at the Testimonials forum, desktop users aren't really concerned with GIMP being overcomplicated. Mostly, they want Ubuntu to work on their hardware and to not have to use the command line. 

If anyone switching to Linux is going to complain about GIMP, it's far more likely that the first thing they're going to say is that it doesn't have the plugins and filters Photoshop does; i.e. that it's not powerful and complex *enough*. 

That said, it's not really a significant inconvenience for people who know they want it, but I don't think this actually helps anything.

I know what you are referring to. I've tried the portable version of GIMP a number of times and have always found that somethings do not seem to work exactly as they do in the, for lack of a better term, non-portable version.

In the case, of clients who are using Ubuntu, there is no guarantee that they have retained a default install. Many companies require that workstations have only the specific applications required for the user of that station to perform their job function. Therefore it is not uncommon for applications to be removed if they are unneeded. I have no control over this except in a purely advisory capacity if the client is one for whom I also do technical support. So from my perspective, the only way for me to be 100% certain I will have the tools I need, is the live CD route. 

Anyway, I'm through even worrying about it. I should have seen this or something similar coming after the decision to replace Pidgen with Empathy. Decisions are being made that are degrading the confidence I have had in Ubuntu as a user since 4.10. I don't feel like I can comfortably even recommend this distro any longer while such decisons are being made. I'll be researching more appropriate alternatives for me and my clients when I get home tonight.

### Post by ezsit on 2009-11-19
> The LiveCD isn't just an installation disk. It's also a showcase for what Ubuntu and linux in general can do. New users who try the live cd will base their opinion on what they see in the first 10 minutes or so. Gimp and openoffice are vital to dragging in the newbies and if Ubuntu's goal is world domination then that's what we need.

Number of problems here. First, Ubuntu should be useful above all else and trying to be a showcase at the expense of more disk space for common tools rather than single purpose tools is not valid. 

Getting rid of GIMP is like not including Ardour for audio editing. Sure, there is no good audio editing tool installed by default, so lets throw in Ardour! GIMP can be discarded as easily as Ardour is NOT included. Heck, the repos are there and anyone can install what they need. 

Second, new users are going to be scared as hell of GIMP. Every person I try to introduce to GIMP goes running for the hills back to Photoshop because GIMP's interface is atrocious and new-user-hostile. Better impressions can be made by leaving GIMP OFF the CD!

GIMP and OpenOffice are not necessary for dragging in new users. If you have to "drag" new users to Ubuntu, they will not stay anyhow. Get the new user reliant upon GIMP and OpenOffice under Windows and THEN, gently introduce Ubuntu. We are not cave men, we do not need to drag users to our den.

Last, who said anything about world domination. If you think that will happen with Linux in our lifetime, you are mistaken. Enjoy using a fringe operating system and the benefits of being on the fringe while it lasts!

---

### Post by aoanla on 2009-11-19
...sometimes, I do wonder what planet the Ubuntu developers are on.

I've been using Ubuntu now since 5.04, and I've never once used F-Spot for anything, including Photoshop. I don't know anyone else with an Ubuntu install who uses F-Spot for managing their photos, either, since it's actually not that good at it.

On the other hand, I frequently use the GIMP for minor photo editing, cropping, and more complex image editing. 
I recently had cause to install a copy of Karmic for my partner, as her harddisk crashed and we didn't have a CD to install Windows XP for her. As a lifelong Windows user, used to using Photoshop for image and photo editing, she found F-Spot an annoying inconvenience, and only really managed to do what she wanted with GIMP.

For this, and other experiences, I find that the thesis presented by the Ubuntu developers (that F-Spot + EoG is a sufficient solution for the majority of users) is not supported by the evidence available to me. If Lucid really does fail to include GIMP as a part of the default install, I will be unable to recommend Ubuntu as a complete linux distribution to others, as it will no longer actually satisfy the requirements of such.

### Post by Gina on 2009-11-19
> **tekstr1der said:**
> If that's the case, why must ubiquity download language packs during installation? This can add considerable time to an install. I suppose I could understand this if you're intending to use an uncommon language, but this happens with the default English US install. While skipping this step is an option, it not only requires user interaction far into the process, but causes an error to be displayed immediately upon first logon.

Having an installer that does not require an Internet connection and mandatory downloads should be a serious priority. If removing GIMP from the ISO can help accomplish this goal, I'm all for it.+1

I noticed that the 9.10 install took considerably longer that previous versions.  This was mainly due to downloading language packs TWICE from the web!!  I live in a rural area and my internet connection reaches 2Mbps at best, often a lot less.  I would hate to think what someone without broadband would think.  Also, I wonder what happens to people with a wireless connection only to their router.  The wireless settings need making before any connection can be made.

Installation should NOT require a net connection IMO and I always thought this was the intention of the CDROM.  So I would agree with ***tekstr1der*** that if removing GIMP from the CD can mean no net connection is used during installation, that **must** be better.  

However, GIMP **must** be replaced with something to edit photos - resize, crop, adjust brightness, contrast, colouring etc and remove redeye - such as gThumb suggested by ***philinux***.

I **am** in favour of keeping the ISO size down and using the CD limit seems a good idea.

### Post by jmmL on 2009-11-19
People have talked about having the GIMP in a "Staff Picks" section of Ubuntu Software Centre. This seems like a good idea.
In addition to this I suggest that a link to install the GIMP could be placed in Eye of Gnome (the default image viewer).

Currently, eog has options under "File > Open with.. > " to open with F-Spot, Firefox and GIMP. The link to GIMP could simply trigger a prompt to install GIMP the first time it is clicked, displaying a helpful screen about what the GIMP is and why a user might want to use it. I'm thinking a window in the style of the ones used in the install slideshow. Obviously after the GIMP is installed clicking this menu option will simply perform as in karmic.

[SIZE=1]Optionally there might also be an option to "don't prompt me again" if you definitely don't want to install the GIMP, ever.[/SIZE]

### Post by den160593 on 2009-11-20
I'm strongly against removing GIMP from the default install. It is, in my opinion, one of the most mature open source applications - it can be a fully fledged Photoshop replacement (especially through GIMPshop but anyhow). Ubuntu, given it's large adoption size, needs to help get more people using the program, encouraging the development of more tutorials by people. It is a lack of tutorials that is GIMP's biggest problem nothing else - and increased adoption (through people looking through the pre-installed programs) will help increase the amount of tutorials available, as writers see that they have a wider audience base. 

I'm all for removing F-Spot though, GIMP can do everything F-Spot can - just better. As for the other things proposed in the blueprint, I'm neutral, but removing GIMP is a big no no.

Right now I am trying to edit an image on my desktop with fspot, right click->open with fspot, and it shows up, but no real editing possible. Try to open inside fspot, and it wants to import folders? I just want to edit an image, not have to import a bunch of images.
Argh, when importing it shows only creation date and not file names? wtf? I'm trying to select a bootchart image (there's 6)  and they all look the same as thumbnails.
I can't insert any text? MSpaint can do that. I can't add any colours or anything simple that mspaint can do? And, it just crashed for no reason with no crash report or anything, just closed when I went into options. That's a bad first impression for me. No wonder I ignore fspot. fspot is a photo manager, not editor, can't do any real image editing, and unable to replace any aspect of gimp other than rotating images and maybe 10 other options.

I don't think gimp is perfect or anything, it has lots of flaws, but it gets the basic or advanced job done for me. fspot can't even do simple jobs for me. It seems to be only for basic photo manipulation(barely), and not any image editing.

### Post by Regenweald on 2009-11-20

The devs got this one right.

### Post by aoanla on 2009-11-21
> **YokoZar said:**
> CD Space and the default install is all about tradeoffs.  It's not about removing GIMP; it's about exposing F-Spot's simple editing mode when a user just wants to do a simple photo editing task.  It's also about making room on the CD for a video editor.

Of course there are space trade-offs when composing a default package list for a CD-based distro.
No-one here has argued with that (although, frankly, I do think that the penetration of DVD readers in the market is way past the point at which a default LiveDVD is a good idea as default).

What most people are upset about is that you're proposing to drop one of the most widely known alternatives to photoshop, one of the most impressive examples of open-source software development... in favour of F-Spot, a photo-management tool that no-one likes (if you look through the thread, you'll see several posters, including me, note that F-Spot being the default photo-manager in Karmic has actually put people we know off using Ubuntu. That's how dire the thing is.).
If you were dropping GIMP and F-Spot for a simple, streamlined image editor with red-eye removal, a lot of the complaints would go away.

### Post by mpt on 2009-11-21
> **ceramicm said:**
> These are all decisions that affect the user, but arbitrary decisions that were made without any user input. In fact, it seems that each of these decisions was made AGAINST the popular opinion of the community. The ubuntuforums community is (to me) one of the greatest assets of Ubuntu, and I think ignoring or opposing it is a mistake on Canonical's part, though probably not one committed intentionally.

Pretending that the decisions are arbitrary just makes you seem less serious. In both the examples you gave, rationales have been provided. Argue against the rationales if you want, but don’t pretend they don’t exist.

As I have said before, people who post in the Ubuntu Forums are a rare and special group of people, and that’s precisely why it’s a bad idea to use you as a guide to what Ubuntu users in general want.

We don’t *ignore* the forums, though the extent to which we follow them is limited by how much spare time we have. But you are a **massively** biased sample compared to Ubuntu users as a whole. You are biased towards highly technical users (otherwise you wouldn’t post on a Web forum at all), you are biased towards whatever previous versions of Ubuntu did (otherwise you’d be less likely to be using it already), and you are biased against anything that has changed since then (otherwise you’d be less likely to bother commenting). Those three biases combine to make you thunderingly wrong sometimes. If you doubt this, remember that around 80 percent of people who contribute to these forums still think that Ctrl Alt Backspace should crash all of a user’s programs by default.

### Post by mpt on 2009-11-21
> **Merk42 said:**
> I'm just curious, what **do** you use as a guide to what Ubuntu users in general want?

Canonical&#8217;s Design team does user research in the form of interviews with current and potential users. For example, my colleagues have recently been interviewing people on how they use social networks. They presented some of their results at UDS this week.

Just like with any other type of contribution to Ubuntu, though, we&#8217;d be delighted to accept statistically reliable information provided by anyone else too. By statistically reliable I mean not &#8220;A poll on the Ubuntu Forums said X&#8221;, but more like &#8220;We surveyed X computer users in Y city about how they use Z, this was our interview script, and these are our results&#8221;.

> **aoanla said:**
> Considering point 2 - in that case, considering that previous versions of Ubuntu shipped F-Spot, how come most of the commenters here hate it? If you believe that we're biased towards the previous state of Ubuntu, then this anomalous reaction should give you pause - as it suggests that the general public (which lacks this bias) should dislike F-Spot even more than we do.

That you like Ubuntu in general doesn&#8217;t necessarily mean you like every detail. :) But yes, it is a concern that some people dislike F-Spot. It is also a concern that some people find Evolution bloated, and that some people find Firefox slow, and that some people find Gimp frustrating. We try to choose the combination of software that will satisfy the most people in 700 megabytes, and we don&#8217;t always have amazing alternatives to choose from.

In the particular case of F-Spot, we will be doing some work during the Lucid cycle to review its usability and make easy fixes (such as letting you edit images without importing them). If you think it would be better to replace F-Spot with another photo management program (such as Shotwell or Solang), the best way to achieve that is to *contribute to that program* to make it a viable replacement in Lucid+1. (This goes double if you think Mono is a bad idea, for whatever reason; the best way to replace it is to produce a better alternative for the applications that use it.)

---

> **mpt]In both the examples you gave, rationales have been provided. Argue against the rationales if you want, but don&#8217 said:**

My point was not that reasons for change did not exist. My point **was** that the community was (is?) not satisfied with these reasons, yet changes were made anyway, despite an unsatisfied community. If you are not satisfying the primary consumers of your product, what are you accomplishing?

However, after reading your posts, I can see a flaw in this view. I previously saw UbuntuForums users as the "community", as "primary consumers" like I mention above. Then I read this:
[QUOTE=mpt]Canonical&#8217;s Design team does user research in the form of interviews with current and potential users.
To me, this makes decisions I before saw as arbitrary now make a lot more sense. If Canonical is conducting market research to improve the Linux desktop, that is very good news indeed. Still, any idea why this body of users who apparently has radically different views from those expressed on UbuntuForums chooses NOT to post here?

Let me clarify, so I do not seem sarcastic or "less serious" as you said. Example: It is VERY common to see threads about a new "Default Theme" in the Testing & Development Subforum here at UbuntuForums. Now, does an issue that pops up so frequently here not come up at all in the Canonical Design Team surveys? It seems to me it must not, since we did not receive a radically new theme for many releases (although things are starting to change).

It seems to me that different things must be important to the survey group and the UbuntuForums community. I do not want to see either community excluded from Canonical's decisions. Not the "potential users" and not the ones of us who have used Ubuntu since the beginning and who are now "massively biased.":)

Having said that, I'm not saying that we shouldn't make our views known - only that we shouldn't get too upset if things don't always got the way we think they should.

### Post by Sense on 2009-11-22
I agree with the reasoning behind the decision to remove The GIMP from  the default installation. It is not used by many people, it is not intended for common users and it takes up precious space.

All people I installed Ubuntu for don't use The GIMP, and there is no integration in F-Spot with the GIMP. No one forces you to use its editing feature, no one forbids you from installing The GIMP.

I would be in favour of adding more translations, but the Desktop team probably has different plans.

### Post by ceramicm on 2009-11-22

By statistically reliable I mean not &#8220;A poll on the Ubuntu Forums said X&#8221;, but more like &#8220;We surveyed X computer users in Y city about how they use Z, this was our interview script, and these are our results&#8221;.[/QUOTE]

### Post by JohnPhys on 2009-11-22
I don't think this is a good idea.  One of the things I like about Ubuntu (and what I believe is a major selling point) is the high quality software, and the amount of software, that comes installed by default as compared to other operating systems.  Applications such as a web browser, multi-protocol chat client, mail & calendering app, cd/dvd burning app, a "jukebox" type app, and an entire office suite make Ubuntu a much more complete experience out of the box than other OS's, where you have to "hunt and peck" after the default install just to have apps that allow you to get anything done.

I'd much rather have fspot removed, as it's not as good of a photo editor as gimp.  While gimp can't play the role of photo manager, gThumb can do this, and I've found gThumb to be *much* easier to use for simple photo-editing tasks (cropping, resizing, etc.) than either fspot or gimp, and I've wondered why it hasn't been included on the LiveCD already.

Also, the "saving space" issue just seems ridiculous to me.  I mean, how many people actually use the "Terminal Server Client" that's included?

Anyway, removing GIMP from the LiveCD and default install just seems like a huge step backwards.  I know people can grab it from the repos, but they have to know it's there, and the LiveCD a nice place to showcase such an application.

*Please*, keep the GIMP on the cd.

### Post by mpt on 2009-11-22
> **ceramicm said:**
> However, after reading your posts, I can see a flaw in this view. I previously saw UbuntuForums users as the "community", as "primary consumers" like I mention above. Then I read this:

[quote=mpt]Canonical’s Design team does user research in the form of interviews with current and potential users.

To me, this makes decisions I before saw as arbitrary now make a lot more sense. If Canonical is conducting market research to improve the Linux desktop, that is very good news indeed. Still, any idea why this body of users who apparently has radically different views from those expressed on UbuntuForums chooses NOT to post here?[/quote]

Sure: Many, probably most, Ubuntu users don’t have the faintest clue that the Ubuntu Forums even exist. And even if they did, posting here isn’t something that would ever interest them. In the same way that you might not post on forums about musicians you listen to, or a car you drive, or a religion you follow, or a movie you’ve watched, or a sports team you support, even though in each case there are many enthusiasts who do. That doesn’t mean you’re weird or wrong, or that they are, or that anyone else is. You just have different passions.

> Let me clarify, so I do not seem sarcastic or "less serious" as you said. Example: It is VERY common to see threads about a new "Default Theme" in the Testing & Development Subforum here at UbuntuForums. Now, does an issue that pops up so frequently here not come up at all in the Canonical Design Team surveys? It seems to me it must not, since we did not receive a radically new theme for many releases (although things are starting to change).

We know Ubuntu needs a new theme, but merely coming to that conclusion does not result in a theme appearing overnight. ;) To do a really good job you need artists, and a vision, and an art director with attention to detail, and plenty of time.

> It seems to me that different things must be important to the survey group and the UbuntuForums community. I do not want to see either community excluded from Canonical's decisions. Not the "potential users" and not the ones of us who have used Ubuntu since the beginning and who are now "massively biased.":)

Yep, it’ll always be a juggling act. If we satisfy only the enthusiasts, we’ll make an operating system that few other people want. But if we piss off the enthusiasts, we’ll lose the people who care most about spreading the word.

> **Gina said:**
> And also, we are a very small sample of users and even counting friends who are less experienced, still a small sample compared with what Canonical appear to be doing.  I could be wrong but as I see it we are not generally market researchers and certainly not in the way Canonical are.  So maybe we should leave it to the experts in that field?

> **Merk42 said:**
> Can they please do market research of GNOME Shell?
Any critique I have is basically answered with "welp you don't have a giant report polling hundreds of people so we don't care what you say"

I don’t want to have given a wrong impression here, so a few clarifications. It’s user research, not market research. We just started this a few months ago. There is no single “survey group”. And we don’t claim to be the sole source of data on who uses Ubuntu and how. Like [I said]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=8361502&postcount=197"), “we’d be delighted to accept statistically reliable information provided by anyone else too”.

For example, at UDS we were pleased to have a couple of talks by Michael Terry, assistant professor at the University of Waterloo, who has done [research on how people use Gimp]("http://www.ingimp.org/statsjam/index.php/Main_Page"). He found, [for example]("http://www.ingimp.org/statsjam/index.php/Command_Counts_in_Sessions"), that the number of different commands used in a session has a median of eight and a mean of eleven.

So it is true that > **hanzomon4 said:**
> Ifor what most people do with photos gimp is over kill
Now that doesn’t, by itself, mean Gimp is bad. After all, for what most people do with documents Microsoft Word is overkill, but it’s still the most popular word processor by far — because as [Joel Spolsky said]("http://www.joelonsoftware.com/articles/fog0000000020.html"), “Everybody uses a *different* set of features”. But it *does* mean it’s possible we can satisfy more people by shipping a bunch of other programs by default instead.

---

### Post by mpt on 2009-11-22
> **confused_user said:**
> I said it before and i'll say it again.  Please (i'm looking at you developers) nevermind about dropping gimp, focus more on making Ubuntu work properly - drivers drivers drivers

worry about applications once you got the OS working properly.

It’s not just you; many, many people have said that before, and they’ll say it again, and they’ll still be mistaken. The people who can work on improving applications are hardly ever the same people who can work on improving drivers, and vice versa. So insisting that one thing be done before something else can begin would achieve nothing except slowing down the whole project.

> **ceramicm said:**
> What about Ubuntu Brainstorm? Does Canonical use information gathered from it? As far as a I can tell, Ubuntu Brainstorm is far more like "A poll on the Ubuntu Forums” than a survey of “X computer users in Y city about how they use Z." Does this make it inaccurate, and therefore ignored in the decision-making process?

If it does play a part, then why don't the people here who support the inclusion of GIMP in the default install vote up such a solution there, rather than continue to debate here? (Opponents could vote it down) If Canonical does abide by the will of users as expressed in Ubuntu Brainstorm, then the issue will be resolved by popular vote. If Canonical does not abide by the will of users as expressed in Ubuntu Brainstorm, then why does it exist?

It’s Brainstorm, not Decisionstorm. As a measurement of popular opinion, it’s probably just as inaccurate as the Forums are. But like the forums, it’s a cool source of ideas. For example, I used ideas from Brainstorm when designing the Ubuntu Software Center. (And the Ubuntu Software Center, in turn, makes it easier to install Gimp if you want it.)

---

### Post by saulgoode on 2009-11-23
> **mpt said:**
> For example, at UDS we were pleased to have a couple of talks by Michael Terry, assistant professor at the University of Waterloo, who has done [research on how people use Gimp]("http://www.ingimp.org/statsjam/index.php/Main_Page"). He found, [for example]("http://www.ingimp.org/statsjam/index.php/Command_Counts_in_Sessions"), that the number of different commands used in a session has a median of eight and a mean of eleven.

So it is true that:
[INDENT]**Originally Posted by hanzomon4**
Ifor what most people do with photos gimp is over kill[/INDENT]
You are over-generalizing the results of the research. "In a session" means a particular invocation of GIMP, and the research does not extrapolate to indicating the myriad ways a user might use GIMP. A user may avail himself of only a half-dozen commands if he is preparing a photo file for printing, and a different half-dozen commands to prepare an image for a website, and a different set for scanning documents, and a different set for drawing cartoons, etc. etc. etc. Nonetheless, it is misguided to take from this that GIMP should only provide a half-dozen commands -- or that it is somehow "overkill" for GIMP to provide commands which support all of these tasks.

> **mpt said:**
> Now that doesn&#8217;t, by itself, mean Gimp is bad. After all, for what most people do with documents Microsoft Word is overkill, but it&#8217;s still the most popular word processor by far &#8212; because as [Joel Spolsky said]("http://www.joelonsoftware.com/articles/fog0000000020.html"), &#8220;Everybody uses a *different* set of features&#8221;. But it *does* mean it&#8217;s possible we can satisfy more people by shipping a bunch of other programs by default instead.
Again, I disagree. While I am a great fan of the Unix philosophy of "do one thing and do it well", this does not apply very well to the case of image manipulation on the desktop because of the large amount of data that must be transferred between the applications and the large amount of data manipulation required just to provide a representative rendering of the image to the user. 

It makes little sense to have a program spend two seconds loading a file (translating it to an internally supported format, applying appropriate color management profiling for the targeted output device, and rendering an appropriate buffer for the display) -- only to spend a few milliseconds performing some task on the image before saving it to a temporary intermediate format which can be passed on to another program which must repeat the process. It is far more sensible to have a single program that is capable of performing all of the necessary operations without needing to employ intermediate files or piping of large amounts of data.

I do think it is reasonable for a photo *management* program to remain distinct from a photo *manipulation* program. Management focuses on the organization of collections and typically does not need to process the entire file as the display of thumbnail previews is in the majority of cases sufficient for identification of the file contents (and color managing the rendered display is not as critical). Further treatment of a particular photo (or set of photos) is not encumbered by invocation of a separate manipulation program as this would entail processing the file in full regardless whether handled internally or externally.

For what it's worth, I do not care whether Canonical chooses to include GIMP in their distribution. I merely feel that it is a misguided decision based on [faulty initial information]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=8360998&postcount=97"), erroneous assumptions, and lack of foresight. 

The GIMP project does not particularly benefit from its inclusion in Ubuntu; Ubuntu benefits from GIMP's inclusion. Apart from Firefox, GIMP is probably the most common Free Software application deployed on Windows and to the extent that Windows users may wish to migrate to GNU/Linux, they will wish to have the software with which they're familiar provided by that distro; especially given that experimenting with a LiveCD is considered a prudent first step in deciding on a distro.

One should also consider that the next release of GIMP will be adding two enhancements that many -- especially Windows users -- have been requesting for a long time: a "single-window mode" and "layer groups". It is not unlikely that users wishing to examine the implementation of these long-sought features will be interested in running a LiveCD rather than modifying their existing setups. This is especially true for Windows users (for whom availability of reliable GIMP releases typically lags that of GNU/Linux by significant periods of time) and tech journalists interested in reviewing GIMP's new features rather than an OS (though a mention that Ubuntu was used during the testing does not to me seem nugatory). 

So do what you want but it is my considered opinion that removing GIMP at this time will prove, ultimately, to the cost of Ubuntu.

---

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2009-11-23
> **CarpKing said:**

There is an unfortunate lack of hope in your words here. The Software Centre exists to defeat that problem. In Lucid, we'll be really close to that goal.

For an end user, he will learn about Software Centre the first time he opens Applications. If he installs Ubuntu himself, he will see Software Centre mentioned prominently during the installation slideshow (more-so than it currently is). Software Centre -> GIMP should be an exciting and obvious path for those interested in image manipulation.

saulgoode: One point about Michael Terry's research is that GIMP targets a *huge* number of audiences, but at the default install we are not reaching for all of them. If we were, there would have to be ufraw, imagemagick, a lot more fonts, tools to make fonts, agave... (If you are one of those users, though, try Ubuntu Studio or install the metapackage).

Indeed, there are solutions, because of course his talks were entirely constructive and awesome. His project is developing an awesome fork called Adaptable GIMP. It provides all GIMP's power with an interface that hides / unhides certain features in favour of tidiness, fitting with the user's current objective.

### Post by YokoZar on 2009-11-24
> **qamelian said:**
> Fair enough, except that I have yet to actually meet face-to-face anyone who actually likes or uses F-pot. And out of a large number of Linux users whom I know personally, I am the only one who actually does video editing on Ubuntu. I certainly can't speak for everyone, but just based on my own personal experience, both as an end-user and as someone providing technical support to over 100 users in my locality, this is a very poor decision. In this case, it is one poor decision too many for me to still feel any confidence in the Ubuntu development process. I seriously doubt that due consideration has been given, because I know that, in the circle of linux users with whom I associate, GIMP is much more widely used than F-spot or video editing software.
I have, on several occasions, attempted to present a new user who wants to do a simple photo editing task with the GIMP.  It's not an experience I'd recommend.

> **ceramicm said:**
> Aren't WE the masses? We, the users of Ubuntu? The majority of which, according the results of this poll, are not impressed by this proposed change?

If not, who are these hypothetical masses? Where are they? Do you have a poll showing their opinion on this issue?
People who come to a forum thread complaining about GIMP and then voting in a poll with slanted wording don't represent Ubuntu's user base.

When we do actual legitimate user studies ([such as InGIMP](http://www.ingimp.org/)), we find that over 90% of GIMP users are only using about 6 functions within it for about 10 minutes total.  That's because the vast majority of users aren't using a free alternative to Photoshop: they're using a free alternative to the Photo Kiosk at the drug store.

> **aoanla said:**
> Of course there are space trade-offs when composing a default package list for a CD-based distro.
It's not just a space trade-off though.  Users want a simple editor when they're doing what should be a simple task - GIMP makes cropping and removing red-eye feel like some sort of herculean effort.

> No-one here has argued with that (although, frankly, I do think that the penetration of DVD readers in the market is way past the point at which a default LiveDVD is a good idea as default).
I support making a LiveDVD (with both i386 and amd64 arches on it) as well, but it's unfortunately not in the cards for this cycle.  Canonical folks tell me that DVDs are also substantially more expensive for ShipIt to support.

> What most people are upset about is that you're proposing to drop one of the most widely known alternatives to photoshop, one of the most impressive examples of open-source software development... in favour of F-Spot, a photo-management tool that no-one likes (if you look through the thread, you'll see several posters, including me, note that F-Spot being the default photo-manager in Karmic has actually put people we know off using Ubuntu. That's how dire the thing is.).
The version of F-Spot you're using (and rightfully complaining about) is not the tool that will be used in Lucid.  In Karmic the simple view and edit mode isn't there - we don't even give the user the option of not using F-Spot as a photo manager before editing.

> If you were dropping GIMP and F-Spot for a simple, streamlined image editor with red-eye removal, a lot of the complaints would go away.This is exactly what Lucid's F-Spot (with a command line switch) is intended to be.  You won't need to use F-Spot for photo management or anything -- there was even a proposal to remove Eye of Gnome as a viewer because it would be so similar to that.

### Post by mpt on 2009-11-24
> **saulgoode said:**
> 
[quote=mpt]So it is true that:
[quote=hanzomon4]Ifor what most people do with photos gimp is over kill[/quote]
You are over-generalizing the results of the research. "In a session" means a particular invocation of GIMP, and the research does not extrapolate to indicating the myriad ways a user might use GIMP. A user may avail himself of only a half-dozen commands if he is preparing a photo file for printing, and a different half-dozen commands to prepare an image for a website, and a different set for scanning documents, and a different set for drawing cartoons, etc. etc. etc.[/quote]

Sure. But we think that scanning a document, and drawing cartoons, are not &#8220;what most people do with photos&#8221;. The implicit conclusion of those in the UDS session (I was there, but didn&#8217;t say much) was that people want to create home movies more often than they want to do those other things.

> [i]Nonetheless, it is misguided to take from this that GIMP should only provide a half-dozen commands -- or that it is somehow "overkill" for GIMP to provide commands which support all of these tasks.
> **mpt]Now that doesn&#8217 said:**
> [/i]

We&#8217;re agreeing with each other here. ;)

[quote]*Again, I disagree. While I am a great fan of the Unix philosophy of "do one thing and do it well", this does not apply very well to the case of image manipulation on the desktop because of the large amount of data that must be transferred between the applications and the large amount of data manipulation required just to provide a representative rendering of the image to the user.*

It would be an interesting design and programming challenge to create the image manipulation equivalent to Firefox &#8212; an image editing program that makes the common things easy, and the rarer things possible through well-integrated and efficient plug-ins. (I know Gimp has plug-ins, but I mean using plug-ins even for functions like blur/sharpen or color alteration.) That would be much better, I think, than providing a simple image editing program and requiring people to install Gimp as soon as they overstep its possibilities. But I don&#8217;t think anything like that exists right now. (As [Mr Picklesworth mentioned]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=8371756&postcount=250"), Michael Terry&#8217;s latest work involves a &#8220;task sets&#8221; interface within Gimp, for simplifying the interface to display the few related functions for a chosen task like cleaning up a scanned document. But I&#8217;m skeptical that it will actually reduce perceived complexity, and in any case it won&#8217;t do anything about CD space consumption.)

> *I do think it is reasonable for a photo [I]management* program to remain distinct from a photo *manipulation* program. Management focuses on the organization of collections and typically does not need to process the entire file as the display of thumbnail previews is in the majority of cases sufficient for identification of the file contents (and color managing the rendered display is not as critical). Further treatment of a particular photo (or set of photos) is not encumbered by invocation of a separate manipulation program as this would entail processing the file in full regardless whether handled internally or externally.[/i]

It&#8217;s more convenient to edit photos in the same window as the rest of the photo manager than in a different window. (That&#8217;s the most visible reason Microsoft&#8217;s OLE 2.0 was better than 1.0, for example.)

> *Apart from Firefox, GIMP is probably the most common Free Software application deployed on Windows*

More common than OpenOffice.org, VLC, Pidgin, and MPlayer? I doubt that.

> **girto said:**
> Would having an extra CD or a DVD release with more applications be a viable solution? With the ability to boot a live "CD" off a USB stick, a slightly larger image could be acceptable.

Ubuntu is already available for [download]("http://www.ubuntu.com/getubuntu/downloadmirrors#dvd") or [purchase]("http://www.amazon.com/s/ref=nb_ss?url=search-alias%3Dsoftware&field-keywords=ubuntu+9.10+dvd") on DVD. But for reasons of cost, download time, or compatibility, most people who get Ubuntu separate from their computer get it on CD.

### Post by Reiger on 2009-11-24
> **saulgoode said:**
> You are [wrong]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=8360998&postcount=97").

Actually I think it is reasonable to infer from some developer talk someone posted (my brain is currently too dead to come online and remember stuff) here that the idea was:

We are pressed for space. GIMP is a bit too big & fat for a Live CD ...
... Let's first try to axe a swathe of docs that you can find online too ....
... If we are still pressed for space...
.... GIMP is still big. Axe it.

So the plan of action was made before GIMP was trimmed down a bit; but not out of misjudgment of actual packaged size.

### Post by ceramicm on 2009-11-24
[QUOTE=andrewabc]How about removing a bunch of the games? Do they take much room? Do people expect simple games with a default OS (solitaire)?[/QUOTE]
There was talk at the UDS about just that. (In fact, at the bottom of the arstechnica page I linked to in my previous post, you can read a little about it.)

I think the general consensus was that yes, some games should be removed, but that a few games with multiplayer functionality (stuff that shows off telepathy, etc.) should be kept.

### Post by ceramicm on 2009-11-24
[QUOTE=23dornot23d]<snip>saying the users are not clever enough to use Gimp<snip>[/QUOTE]
Respectfully, I do not at all think that is what the Ubuntu developers intend to say.

The Ubuntu developers want to give the best experience possible to the most users possible. They believe that most users prefer a streamlined interface filled with intuitive and useful features. They know that users who require more advanced features can generally find and install a program that has them (ie. GIMP).

The fact that most users will never use half of GIMP's features hardly means those users aren't "clever." The developers are not trying to be condescending, but helpful to a majority of Ubuntu's users.

By the way, I too think face recognition would be a cool feature to have in Ubuntu. This has been worked on in the past as a Google Summer of Code project. Faces can be detected in photos, but the ability to match those faces up (ie. selecting all the photos containing "Mark") does not yet exist. This is not "original," however, (I don't know why you thought it was). Picasa and iPhoto have both had this feature for a while now.

Here's a couple blog posts I found about face recognition in F-Spot, in case you are interested:
[http://apart-dev.blogspot.com/2007/07/svn-rev-21.html](http://apart-dev.blogspot.com/2007/07/svn-rev-21.html)
[http://www.pittle.org/weblog/f-spot-face-detection-2_433.html](http://www.pittle.org/weblog/f-spot-face-detection-2_433.html)

---

### Post by ceramicm on 2009-11-25
[QUOTE=openuniverse]i'd love to know if the gimp community feels betrayed by this.[/QUOTE]

According to [this]("http://arstechnica.com/open-source/news/2009/11/giving-up-the-gimp-is-a-sign-of-ubuntus-mainstream-maturity.ars") arstechnica article,
> The GIMP developers who have expressed views on the subject seem to agree with Ubuntu's plan. Sven Neumann, a respected GIMP developer and the author of the GIMP Pocket Reference, voiced approval on the GIMP developer mailing list.

"That is pretty much in-line with our product vision. GIMP is a high-end application for professionals. It is not the tool that you would advise every user to use for their casual photo editing," he wrote.

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2009-11-25
23dornot23d: Just out of curiousity, why DO you scatter insane series of periods, commas and dashes throughout your writing?

And yes, F-Spot works with the portrait / landscape meta data.

The import thing is weird. That's one feature which REALLY needs work. I often want it to move stuff that's stored locally to my Photos directory (which, personally, I think it manages quite well) but it doesn't do that. Doesn't copy them, either; it just links to the current absolute path, wherever that is. (Then the use of absolute paths is similarly ridiculous).

Also, it copies all your photos from a camera to the hard drive as the first step in importing stuff, before it presents you with any feedback. (That's the enormously long "loading" step). So, if it crashes during the operation, you get a ton of loose image files floating around that F-Spot created and then forgot about. Meanwhile, if you only wanted F-Spot to import a couple of pictures from the card, it wastes a ton of time copying over all of them and then deleting the unwanted ones. (At least that's how I understand it; haven't looked at the source code, but I have watched the terrifyingly weird behaviour via Nautilus).

Very unpleasant. That's one feature that needs some serious love (in fact, a do-over) if there are any takers. The only good thing about that part of the program is the many ways you can get to it.

---

### Post by andrewabc on 2009-11-25
> **ronacc said:**
> you have to understand their definition of edit . it includes only crop resize and rotate . painting and adding text are too complex for the average user .

FSPOT can't even resize properly!
There is no resize by percentage (or pixels).

If someone take a 10megapixel picture, it is unlikely they want to upload 5mb of data to send the picture as an email or facebook. They can try, and maybe facebook auto resizes, but what the user should do is reduce size by percentage. It's not that difficult for a dumb user to "make the picture 30% of original size" instead of having to upload the original file.

Many emails only have 1-5mb limit per email so trying to send a bunch of 5mb pictures not resized it difficult.

And what if I go on vacation to some Cuban resort and for some reason wanted to put text onto the picture stating the location (name of building). Sure filename can be renamed, but maybe they have the picture in one of those digital frames and want people to see the pic and read the location (or names of people). Those are options dumb normal people need.

Please don't dismiss simple text/paintbrush and other options as "too advanced" or unneeded.
dumb users have used mspaint for a decade (or other more advanced stuff that came with computer). If they were to switch to ubuntu and there is no simple image editing program by default to accomplish this then fspot is a failure as the default image editing program. Mostly because currently fspot is not an image editor. It tries to be a friendly camera picture editor/organizer, but does so poorly, and I'm not sure how they plan on fixing all the problems in 3-4 months.

The theory is that we don't want users damaging the original image from their cameras, so formatting an image to fit on the web is only done for that one operation. Makes sense, given its specific role and audience: managing photographs for people who want to organize and share photos.

F-Spot has been in Ubuntu since Jaunty, if I recall correctly, and its target audience is not changing.

### Post by andrewabc on 2009-11-25
> **Mr. Picklesworth said:**
> You've essentially proven, at this point, that your argument has not been researched at all. F-Spot's exporters all provide the option to resize images. The theory is that we don't want users damaging the original image from their cameras, so formatting an image to fit on the web is only done for that one operation. Makes sense, given its specific role and audience: managing photographs for people who want to organize and share photos.

F-Spot has been in Ubuntu since Jaunty, if I recall correctly, and its target audience is not changing.

Ok, so I go to export file to folder, and it has "resize to x pixels" I'm slightly confused as to how resizing to a pixel gives a size. Images are length by height, how exactly do I resize by only one pixel input?
I just exported to desktop at 55 pixels. It created a folder, with 5 folders in it and two files. where the hell is the picture I exported to resize it?
When I try to open some of the images in the folders it says
"Error interpreting JPEG image file (Not a jpeg file: starts with 0x89 0x50)"

Ok, so now I realize I have to select "save the files only" instead of default "create standalone webgallery" (wtf is that?), and it resized it to 32x55pixels. So I guess the pixel input changes the height and adjusts width accordingly. Great that it let me know that. Still no % resize, the person would have to know in the first place how many pixels are in height, and then calculate on their own how big they want it. So they have to finish exporting it, then see it is too big/small, then have to open fspot and do same thing again since there is no undo button.

Let's say I want to export to flickr, and I have really big image from camera. I want to resize it to a smaller image. I get to exporter and it has the "resize to x pixels". It doesn't list anywhere original size in pixels in the exporter, so how the hell is a dumb user supposed to know what pixel amount to put in and know that it affects height? There appears to be no preview of what the pixel resize will look like, so they click "export" and it gets uploaded to flickr and is the wrong size (too big/small).

> what you've essentially proven is that you think the average user is too stupid to know the difference between "save" and "save as".
Not sure if intended for me, but fspot doesn't have "save and save as". Which most other programs use.

Also, I click "import source" and select one of my partitions its starts automatically importing and freezes (had to kill it) fspot. Why doesn't it ask what folders in the partition to look into instead of trying to search the entire partition?

Sorry but the more research I do into fspot the worse it looks.

### Post by ronacc on 2009-11-26
ECMA standard does Not mean open source , from wikipedia .
```
Criticism

Although the C# language definition and the CLI are standardized under ISO and Ecma standards which provide reasonable and non-discriminatory licensing protection from patent claims, Microsoft uses C# and the CLI in its Base Class Library (BCL) which is the foundation of its proprietary .NET framework, and which provides a variety of non-standardized classes (extended I/O, GUI, web services, etc). Some cases where Microsoft patents apply to standards used in the .NET framework are documented by Microsoft and the applicable patents are available on either RAND terms or through Microsoft's Open Specification Promise which releases patent rights to the public,[19] but there is some concern and debate as to whether there are additional aspects patented by Microsoft which are not covered, which may deter independent implementations of the full framework. Microsoft has also agreed not to sue open source developers for violating patents in non-profit projects for the part of the framework which is covered by the OSP.[20] Microsoft has agreed not to enforce patents relating to Novell products against Novell's paying customers[21] with the exception of a list of products that do not explicitly mention C#, .NET or Novell's implementation of .NET (The Mono Project),[22]. However Novell maintains that Mono does not infringe any Microsoft patents.[23] Microsoft has also made a specific agreement not to enforce patent rights related to the Moonlight browser plugin, which depends on Mono, provided it is obtained through Novell.[24]

In a note posted on the Free Software Foundation's news website in June 2009, Richard Stallman warned that he believes "Microsoft is probably planning to force all free C# implementations underground some day using software patents" and recommended that developers avoid taking what he described as the "gratuitous risk" associated with "depend[ing] on the free C# implementations".[25]. The Free Software Foundation later reiterated its warnings[26], claiming that the extension of Microsoft Community Promise to the C# and the CLI ECMA specifications[27] would not prevent Microsoft from harming Open-Source implementations of C#, because many specific Windows libraries included with .NET or Mono were not covered by this promise.
```

extracted from [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language))

---

### Post by saulgoode on 2009-11-26
> **Merk42 said:**
> The [same thing will happen in GIMP for 2.8](http://www.gimpusers.com/tutorials/gimp-2-8-new-features.html), but I guess it's okay when they do it.
The issue being addressed by GIMP's export interface is somewhat different. It is not about keeping the original file intact, but about saving your data to file formats which do not preserve all of the information that was being edited in GIMP. 

For most image formats reloading a saved image will not completely get you back to where you were when the image was saved (analogous to how saving a word processing document to a text file will discard formatting information). GIMP is trying to address that this being such a common scenario in typical image editing workflows, it is obnoxious to keep popping up warning dialogs and asking the user what to do each time he saves the image.

---

### Post by 23meg on 2009-11-26
> **ronacc said:**
> what I meant was that "save" is going to overwrite the original with the changed (some apps MAY make a backup ) , "save as " saves the file under a new name and leaves the original alone .

And how do you expect new computer users to discover that difference without the potential for data loss? 

And how do you deal with the accumulation of version after version of "saved" and "save as'd" copies of file after file after file? How do you deal with a new Ubuntu user whose goal is to resize a photo before sending it as an attachment or uploading it to a photo sharing service ending up with four copies of what is essentially the same photo labeled as "IMG_1449.jpg", "IMG_1449_resized.jpg", "IMG_1449" and "IMG_1449 (copy).jpg"? How do you deal with that problem scaled to thousands of photos, coupled with poor knowledge of Ubuntu defaults and little experience with computers in general?

You can't, unless you get down to the root of the problem, and get rid of, in chronological order:

[list]
[*] the distinction between "save" and "save as" in favor of built-in versioning


[*] the concepts of "saving", "importing", "exporting" files at all, in favor of object indexers and catalogs provided by default


[*] the concept of "files" as the basic unit of data organization, in favor of "objects".
[/list]

The constant labeling of people who believe in getting rid of metaphors based on aging technical implementation specifics that non-technical users are neither interested nor knowledgeable in, that don't fit and don't scale in real life use, in favor of those designed in a human-centric way with specific goals in mind as "those who think the average user is too stupid" is getting sickening. Please stop it. 

If you have proper arguments as to why wrestling with files, filesystems and folder hierarchies is still a good idea for Ubuntu's audience in 2009, present them. If you have none, be so kind as not to mischaracterize the positions of people who think differently in this and similar discussions.

---

Criticism

Although the C# language definition and the CLI are standardized under ISO and Ecma standards which provide reasonable and non-discriminatory licensing protection from patent claims, Microsoft uses C# and the CLI in its Base Class Library (BCL) which is the foundation of its proprietary .NET framework, and which provides a variety of non-standardized classes (extended I/O, GUI, web services, etc). ### Post by Reiger on 2009-11-26
> **23meg said:**
> [list]
[*] the distinction between "save" and "save as" in favor of built-in versioning


[*] the concepts of "saving", "importing", "exporting" files at all, in favor of object indexers and catalogs provided by default


[*] the concept of "files" as the basic unit of data organization, in favor of "objects".
[/list]


There is certainly technical merit but there are also a couple of interesting &#8216;real world&#8217; consequences arising from this approach.


First in order to do (1) people must have a notion of &#8216;project&#8217; or at least some unit of work that transcends a specific version of a specific file/result-on-disk. This essentially requires a different mindset because you quickly run into such questions as:
(a) Is this a throw-away experiment? And if it is should it be versioned _at all_?
(b) What constitutes a commit to the project? Mere file saving may not be enough if (a) applies.
(c) Deliberate different versions of the same file, aka branches of the project. And how to merge back if the data is not text? Typically the tools have a means of object manipulation that allows for high level merging that is not readily apparent from the data format (i.e. ripping/copying audio-streams from MPEG files is not as simple on the file-level/data-level as it is on the conceptual level when working with tool sets that understand the [entire] file format). 

For automatic versioning to work people must either work with a dedicated version control system; or their storage system must itself be a version control system. In the former case we are no longer quite the average user (there's an awful lot of concepts heading your way at alarming speeds at first when you do go down that road). In the latter case we end up with a generic system in which the user is unaware of the versioning and unable to influence it much (unless he/she delves into the technicalities but that is much like fiddling with mount options today). Consequentially; in the latter case we have a very real problem: binary blobs cannot be treated efficiently, yet it are those blobs that the user is probably going to be most interested in: movies, audio clips, pictures (photo's).

There is no option but a full sized copy (yes: that is what typically happens when you commit a binary blob to versioning) and we still do not have a transparent portable means of identifying a commit -- a simple save is not always quite the same as a commit.

These are at the present technical challenges: not arguments why versioning is a bad idea. (Indeed I'd agree that versioning is a good idea.)

Secondly a catalog and indexers... That sort of exists and makes good sense. But on the other hand completely doing away with &#8216;save&#8217;, &#8216;export&#8217;, &#8216;import&#8217;? The key here is that the world does not stop at the electronic door of my OS/computer. There is the wider world to comply with and the wider world might have very different opinions about data and formats and what makes sense in general. As such it would be impolite to send xyz files to people I know that they do not have a program for xyz; it would be only common courtesy to send them a common-format equivalent instead. E.g. export to JPEG or PNG files for &#8216;have a look; is this any good?&#8217; type of e-mail.

Third issue ties in with:

> If you have proper arguments as to why wrestling with files, filesystems and folder hierarchies is still a good idea for Ubuntu's audience in 2009, present them. If you have none, be so kind as not to mischaracterize the positions of people who think differently in this and similar discussions.

Yes I happen to think that the concept of &#8216;files&#8217; as well as the concept of &#8216;file system hierarchy&#8217; is something the users better understand ASAP. I happen to think that people kind of learn this rather quickly once introduced to any large-ish library but there you go.

Not because it is so very pleasant to work with. But more because it is a very practical means of making ad-hoc sense of something. 

In some way or another the more productive or at least the more organized users will end up grouping some &#8217;objects&#8217; together with some others in a way of saying &#8216;this bunch is logically separate from that lot&#8217;. Currently this means creating 2 directories and putting &#8216;this bunch&#8217; in one; and &#8216;that lot&#8217; in the other. In other words; I think that in some way or another you end up with a new file/directory structure anyways because file/directory structure is not just OS/system layout: it is a means of insulating a few items from another few so we can make sense of the whole of complexity of all our data -- a few items at a time.

For another example of this: do you only work on one single project? Or do you work on multiple projects? And if you do work on one single project is there not some kind of sub-project hierarchy in the big one?

Finally file/directory type serialization is much more portable than object serialization. Precisely because it leaves the task of object-serialization to the realm of applications that do know how to deal with the resulting file anyways. In a way or another we already have this &#8216;list of objects&#8217;: that is what a file is.

But storing raw objects also means having to store their interdependencies and relations which is not portable; in much the same way you cannot use the raw disk layout of a ext2 partition and treat it as if it were ZFS.. that is because where file/directory is a logical concept easily replicated across a wide array of systems; a given disk layout or on-disk objects are concrete pieces and a non-portable pieces of data layout/definition.

---

### Post by ceramicm on 2009-11-26

Now I have two copies of this photo, taking up twice as much space on my hard drive. The first copy is very dark, virtually useless, and I just want to get rid of it. Sometimes I *want* "destructive" editing.

Is there a way to do something like this in F-Spot? To mark a modified version of a file as the best version, and to remove all the other versions and unclutter my hard drive?
